DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "dd9ccfde-497c-417d-87ce-2e89a3529a58", "absorb_synth_ids": ["f92fe644-5f2c-4e3b-9720-db2426bb2ecb"], "rationale": "Both features implement the same declarative permission/access-control decorator pattern (including @PartialAccess) in Ts.ED backends across ATOACore and AtoaIntegration — a change to the shared permission contract (e.g. new permission type, decorator interface, partial-access semantics) would require coordinated updates in both repos."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "c1ef3a8b-a762-4edb-8e01-707427bae348", "absorb_synth_ids": ["ff38ab10-e78a-4b45-8e76-938dde011638"], "rationale": "Both features model internal Atoa staff accounts with elevated privileges distinct from merchant/customer accounts, sharing the same access-control and authentication contract — a change to staff identity rules (e.g. adding a new privilege tier or auth field) would require coordinated updates to both the AtoaEmployee entity and the DashboardUser entity."}

silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "a0444b75-1b26-41a4-a0ed-bb2a6ab6e9e1", "absorb_synth_ids": ["e9c01afb-8df4-42d8-a829-8cd1340f4820"], "rationale": "Both features implement the same AWS S3 file upload/retrieval capability behind an identical service interface (AwsS3Service.ts), meaning any change to the S3 SDK version, bucket configuration, pre-signed URL logic, or storage contract would require coordinated updates in both repos."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
```json
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "38d0ab36-6d58-44ff-89f6-ade138dabb6f", "absorb_synth_ids": ["a57e9f1d-f3dc-4e23-853b-ad3f7b82b60a", "e14467b6-c6c4-4867-9319-d4481e6b86a4"], "rationale": "Layout's BusinessSelectorDropdownContent and CurrentBusinessSelectorCard are rendered within the PageAppBar navigation area (same end-user navigation chrome), and Composables' useAppBarConfig directly configures PageAppBar.vue — a change to the app bar structure requires coordinated updates to all three."}
```

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "74ade0cf-e4f7-4581-9a0e-3fb7f67243c5", "absorb_synth_ids": ["914092c7-2d96-4ceb-92ba-361502f89c42", "b5544fee-4cc6-4c2c-bad7-dffbdbc08454"], "rationale": "All three implement the same end-user product capability — OTP generation, delivery, and validation for authentication — sharing the same payload contract (OTP codes, JWT response, rate-limiting semantics), and any change to OTP schema, attempt limits, or delivery channel would require coordinated changes across all three."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "7168d481-7b96-417d-9ea0-c4d85a896835", "absorb_synth_ids": ["7d9e5140-4f48-4d49-ae8d-4a0341feba42"], "rationale": "Both features describe the identical product capability — scheduled batch polling of invoice/webhook status records and dispatching transactional emails to merchants/consumers — sharing the same code paths (invoice/dbFunctions.py, webhookEmail/dbFunctions.py, webhookStatusEmail.py) and the same payload contract between invoice data and webhook email notifications."}

     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "4f9733a0-d7df-4929-82ac-f309d7027d70", "absorb_synth_ids": ["6cc629a1-7956-438e-a6b2-2a9e6db6b3b4"], "rationale": "The Home Dashboard directly consumes TransactionStatsService for its stats cards and area graph, and any change to the transaction statistics contract (fields, periods, aggregation logic) requires coordinated updates to the dashboard rendering components — they are impact-coupled across the same frontend codebase."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "42e5783b-a6c5-4a4c-a8ee-3196bfe038f3", "absorb_synth_ids": ["c78caa39-d39c-45cc-b5de-277cda262dd7"], "rationale": "Both features implement the same SaaS billing engine in the same repo (ATOABatch), sharing identical code paths (addon/dbFunctions.py, addon/addonFeatureLimit.py, addon/getAddonBillingAmount.py, pax/__init__.py) and describing the same product capability — monthly invoice generation covering subscription fees, addon charges, and PAX terminal billing — so any change to the billing logic requires coordinated changes across both."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "3d441e64-d37f-4888-959b-ed5e401eafa8", "absorb_synth_ids": ["61043111-8ca7-495e-8434-0dd4c9596190", "f492adeb-8fd8-47b8-8e4e-75afdbe492b6"], "rationale": "All three implement the same internal Slack notification capability (posting operational alerts to Slack channels via webhooks), share identical payload contracts (webhook URL, message body, channel routing), and a change to Slack message format or webhook configuration would require coordinated updates across all three repos."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "3af5f4c9-552c-4235-bd66-d0fb8200e9d6", "absorb_synth_ids": ["c90215a6-41bc-437b-b249-c8dbb5c81fc8"], "rationale": "Both features implement the same scheduled background task execution capability across two repos (ATOAPayment and ATOACore), sharing identical architecture (cron server entry point, ScheduledTasksExecutorService, environment-variable-driven task selection) and overlapping domain concerns (payment expiry, reconciliation, cleanup), meaning any change to the task registration contract, executor lifecycle, or cron server pattern would require coordinated changes in both repos."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
```json
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "139523a0-e498-4c97-814a-cd6799c8ba78", "absorb_synth_ids": ["42fddf21-0571-4fa0-823a-ed45b69e5add", "7eaacb09-b021-4357-a8e6-750a439d1c48", "9c12b59a-573e-4d30-9b14-617d2f34bb3d"], "rationale": "All four features implement the same product capability — transactional email delivery (OTP, payment, account events) — across two repos and multiple layers: the canonical provides HTML template content (AtoaCommunication), C3 is the SendGrid provider that dispatches those templates in the same repo, C2 is the template email service in ATOACore that builds and sends the same event types, and C1 provides the SendGrid API data models consumed by C2; adding any new transactional email event type requires coordinated changes across all four."}
```

  Repo:        ATOABatch (processor, python)
  Title:       Feature: Integration Transaction Retry
  Description: A batch job that retries failed POS integration transactions across multiple integration types — Pointone, Tevalis, EposNow, and generic integrations — and also processes failed card payment webhook logs. For EposNow refund transactions it checks the live payment status before retrying, and for Tevalis it recalculates outstanding amounts and sets table-close flags. DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "2cdbf12b-bcf8-4733-9ba3-0ae476bd89bc", "absorb_synth_ids": ["5e775731-5fc0-44ba-be69-079b9480d703"], "rationale": "Both features describe the same scheduled batch jobs (integrationRetryTransaction.py and checkForPendingTransaction.py) in the same repo, sharing identical code paths and implementing the same product capability — retrying failed integration transactions and resolving pending ones — making any change to one inseparable from the other."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
```json
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "0926669c-cc95-4e7a-8036-a0fa565eb2f2", "absorb_synth_ids": ["96435563-9f66-4d50-add1-3fe55a87d201"], "rationale": "Candidate 1's WebhookController receives and routes inbound Rapyd card payment webhooks — including refund notification webhooks — directly into the refund processing pipeline that the canonical owns; both share the Rapyd refund webhook payload contract, so any change to that contract (event type, payload shape, routing key) requires coordinated changes across both controllers, making them impact-coupled. Candidate 2 (Card Dispute Management) handles a distinct product domain (chargebacks) with its own webhook events, transaction records, and email notifications and is not impact-coupled to refund processing."}
```

Rationale: All five features implement the same 'merchant-configured custom fields collected at payment time' capability across layers: the backend (ATOACore) defines and persists the page/field schema, the Vue3 frontend renders and validates those fields for web consumers, and the Flutter POS app renders the same merchant-defined fields for in-person payments — a schema change to custom field types or the payment page config API would require coordinated updates across all four repos.

  • atoa_paxFeature: Payment Formabsorbed

  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "25d76031-d64e-46de-865c-784cb74a5913", "absorb_synth_ids": ["8e5c2101-f920-4e89-9d1f-d0f6f8cb0101"], "rationale": "Both features implement the same IAM product capability—JWT Bearer validation against the shared atoa_credentials database, internal service-to-service token verification, and role-based access control—meaning any change to the shared token contract, error codes, or auth context shape would require coordinated changes in both repos."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "1a426325-c369-4e1e-ba51-144f13ce993b", "absorb_synth_ids": ["329310cc-f535-4376-98be-eded4313a891"], "rationale": "Both features implement the same open banking AIS product capability via Yapily — OAuth consent initiation, callback handling, and bank account/transaction data sync — across two backend repos that share the same Yapily AIS contract, meaning changes to consent flow or account data schema require coordinated updates in both."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "0e5eaf5b-7f43-4d1d-be5f-ecf58b3b62d6", "absorb_synth_ids": ["9227cd55-7882-47b2-9271-51aac65345d5"], "rationale": "Both features describe the same shared external messaging/notification infrastructure (ExternalMessagingService + NotificationTopicCodeEnum) with identical code paths — any change to the messaging models, enum values, or service interface requires coordinated updates across both, making them fully impact-coupled."}

DECISION RULES

The purpose of merging is **impact analysis**: when a developer is
about to make a change, knowing which repos will be touched. So the
test is not "are these features the same?" but "would a typical
change to one require coordinated changes to the other?"

1. IMPACT-COUPLED → MERGE. Two features are impact-coupled when a
   typical change to one would likely require coordinated changes to
   the other. Strong signals:
   - One sends a message / payload / event that the other receives or
     consumes (e.g. backend FCM sender + frontend service worker
     receiver — changing payload format breaks both).
   - One exposes an HTTP endpoint that the other calls (UI form +
     backend handler — changing request schema breaks both).
   - One writes to a queue/topic that the other reads.
   - They share a contract: schema, event format, routing key, error
     code set, retry semantics.
   - They implement the **same product capability across layers**
     (e.g. "OTP request UI" + "OTP generation/validation backend" →
     same product feature: consumer authentication).

2. SHARED INFRASTRUCTURE WITHOUT PRODUCT COUPLING → DO NOT merge.
   Generic utilities that happen to be referenced by both but where
   neither implements a specific feature behaviour (logging, error
   handling, base classes, generic helpers).

3. DIFFERENT PRODUCT FEATURES → DO NOT merge. No shared product
   capability and no payload/contract coupling.

Important reframings:

- "Independently useful" or "could be replaced separately" is NOT a
  reason to keep features apart. A frontend button and the backend
  endpoint it POSTs to are also "independently replaceable" with
  stubs — but they are still impact-coupled. The right test is
  whether a typical change requires coordinated work, not whether
  they could in principle be swapped.

- "Send" and "receive" of the same message are NOT separate
  features. They share a payload contract — changing the payload
  schema breaks both. That's the textbook impact-coupling case.
  MERGE them.

- Cross-layer pairs (frontend ↔ backend ↔ processor) implementing
  the same product feature are the highest-value merge targets. The
  whole point is to surface "this BUD touches both layers" without
  the developer guessing.

When in doubt, prefer to MERGE. For impact analysis, a missed merge
silently hides risk (the developer doesn't know to update the other
repo). A wrong merge is visible and easy to split later. The cost
asymmetry favours merging.

Signals (strongest → weakest):
- Explicit contract overlap (HTTP route hits endpoint, FCM sender ↔
  receiver of same topic, queue producer ↔ consumer) → STRONGEST.
- Tags overlap (both "auth", both "payments", both "notifications")
  → strong.
- Title overlap with shared product noun ("OTP", "Magic Link",
  "Push Notifications") → strong, especially across layers.
- Description string overlap → moderate (vocabulary often differs
  across layers but real same-capability pairs share noun phrases).

RESPOND WITH ONLY A SINGLE JSON OBJECT, NO PROSE BEFORE OR AFTER.

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "0e57f7e3-c729-49c0-8300-e2a9e999579a", "absorb_synth_ids": ["37578506-9bd1-41a7-be92-f7f41ff168df"], "rationale": "Both features implement the same CSV export capability for merchant/transaction data across two backend repos (ATOAPayment and ATOACore), sharing the same product contract — CSV column configuration, formatting utilities, and export job lifecycle — such that changes to export format, column schema, or streaming behaviour would require coordinated updates in both repos."}
no_match Feature: Core Integration Management cluster size: 2 · absorbed: 0
Rationale: The canonical manages the catalogue of available integrations (what integrations exist, their types, payment eligibility), while the candidate manages merchant-to-integration relationships (which merchants have connected which integrations, with what credentials) — these are distinct product capabilities at different layers of the domain model, and a typical change to one (e.g. adding a new integration type) would not require coordinated changes to the other (e.g. how merchants link to integrations).

If you find a SAME-CAPABILITY match:
{"action": "merge", "canonical_synth_id": "<source-id>",
 "absorb_synth_ids": ["<candidate-id-1>", ...],
 "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

If no candidate is SAME-CAPABILITY:
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "<one sentence>"}

The "canonical_synth_id" should always be the SOURCE feature's id.
{"action": "no_match", "rationale": "Although both features monitor pending Open Banking transactions and share infrastructure (batch job pattern, DB functions, Slack utils), they target distinct payment rails — the canonical handles standard PIS settlement polling while the candidate handles AIS account-information transactions — meaning a change to settlement polling logic would not require coordinated changes to AIS transaction processing and vice versa."}
